# Wonder Man — UATU INTELLIGENCE DOSSIER

*Full-existence intelligence briefing. Independent, evidence-based assessment — the benevolent inverse of total surveillance.*


## Executive Brief (BLUF)

# EXECUTIVE BRIEF (BLUF)

**File:** WILLIAMS, SIMON — alias WONDER MAN
**Section:** 0.0 / Executive Brief — Bottom Line Up Front
**Classification:** UATU EYES / Understanding Protocol
**Aggregation window:** Pre-transformation civilian life through current ionic-veteran period — full longitudinal record.

---

## 0.1 Bottom Line

Simon Williams is a sincerely decent, structurally hollow man whose every substantive feature — his body, his powers, his face, his "soul-print," his voice, his livelihood, even the felt continuity of being himself from one morning to the next — was authored by someone else. He is the only Marvel-tier figure in the active operational environment whose somatic substrate, psychological substrate, and professional substrate are *all three* borrowed instruments. He is, in the cleanest possible formulation: **a man who cannot reliably locate a self underneath the performances of a self, and who copes by performing harder.**

He is not dangerous in the conventional adversary sense. He is dangerous in the *instrumentation* sense — a high-yield asset with a structurally compromised identity core, who has been re-pointed at the Avengers (the institution he is most identified with) at least once already, by no manipulator other than himself. The probability that he will be re-pointed again, by himself or another, is non-trivial and rises with each successive death-and-return cycle. The trend on his identity coherence is monotonic downward. The trend on his moral floor — striking, and the single most important countervailing fact in this file — is essentially flat. He keeps reconstituting around the same ethical instinct: at the wire, he will not be the villain in his own story.

That instinct has saved his life, the Avengers' lives, and the planet's continued occupancy of the third orbital position more than once. It is also the only reliably load-bearing element of his personality. Everything else is negotiable, performable, and revisable under sufficient relational pressure.

---

## 0.2 Who He Is, At The Deepest Level

Strip away the costume eras (red-and-green safari, the W-chest, the leather-jacket Hollywood phase, the energy-form, the recent restraint-coded variants); strip away the team affiliations (Zemo's pawn, Avenger, West Coast Avenger, Force Works lead, Revenger, Uncanny Avenger, returnee); strip away the powerset oscillations (Class-100 strongman, flier, ionic discharger, discorporated revenant); strip away the romantic résumé (the Vision-shaped triangle around Wanda, the post-Wanda half-attachments). What remains is a Paterson, NJ kid who was the *second* son of a man who measured his children by output, who watched his older brother become the temperamentally dominant one without effort, who inherited a company because his father died rather than because he had earned it, who lost that company to a smarter man his own age (Stark), who chose fraud over admission of failure, who got caught, and who has spent every subsequent decade — across literal deaths — trying to be a person his father would have respected and his brother would not have despised.

The deepest layer is **a child of conditional regard who never resolved the condition.** Every adult attachment recapitulates the original test: *am I enough this time?* The answer is structurally never yes for long enough to stabilize him, because the regard he requires must come from outside, and outside conditions change. Wanda's love organized him for a period; Wanda's withdrawal disorganized him for a longer one. The Avengers' acceptance organized him; the Avengers' implicit ranking of him below Thor, Iron Man, and the Vision disorganized him. The audience's applause organizes him in the short term but cannot follow him into combat or bed.

He is, in the religious sense the canon does not use but supports, **a man with no interior witness.** Most people carry inside themselves a stable observer who can confirm "you exist, you are you, you did the thing." Simon does not. The observer's seat is empty, and he keeps trying to fill it with someone he can see — a lover, a director, a team, a brother, an audience, even, in the Revengers period, a *cause*. Each occupant eventually leaves or proves insufficient, and the seat is empty again, and he destabilizes — sometimes psychologically, sometimes literally, his ionic form sloughing photons.

---

## 0.3 The Through-Line

There is one sentence that describes his entire publication history without distortion:

> *Simon Williams is a man whose existence is repeatedly authored by others, who keeps choosing — at the last possible moment, at maximum cost to himself — not to be the instrument they made him.*

Every era is a variant of this sentence.

- **Pre-transformation:** authored by his father (heir, executive), revises by embezzling (badly, and is caught).
- **Avengers #9:** authored by Zemo (assassin, Trojan horse), revises at the last possible moment by turning on Zemo and apparently dying for it.
- **Resurrection arc:** authored by his own trauma (the death-phobic shell), revises by forcing himself back into combat against the Wrecking Crew.
- **Grim Reaper arc:** authored by his brother's hatred (the "soulless thing" he is told he is), revises by refusing to kill the brother despite decades of provocation and burying him with grief intact.
- **Vision-template arc:** authored by his own engrams in another man's head (the husband-shaped shadow), revises *badly* — the Vision-refusal in VisionQuest is the one era where the last-minute revision goes the wrong way and he chooses jealousy. This is the load-bearing failure of his moral life. He has not metabolized it.
- **Wanda romance:** authored by being the man she chose, briefly, revises by losing her and not pursuing reprisal.
- **Force Works death:** authored by the team-leader role he never quite fit, revises by dying well.
- **Discorporated period:** *authored entirely* by Wanda's unconscious longing — he is, literally, only present when she wants him to be — and there is no revision available, because there is no Simon distinct enough to do the revising. This is the most ontologically naked period in the file.
- **Revengers turn:** authored by his own grievance-ideology (he becomes, briefly, his own Zemo), revises by eventually returning, apologizing in substance if not always in words, and re-attaching to the Avengers.
- **Late-period oscillation:** authored by his trauma, his pacifist commitments, his Hollywood persona, and his unresolved Wanda-and-Vision triangulation, in rotating proportion. Revisions are smaller in amplitude. The man is tired.

The through-line is not the failure. The through-line is the **revision** — the fact that he keeps doing it, even when it costs him his body, his reputation, his lover, his standing, and his life. The shame substrate is the engine. He cannot live with being the villain in his own story, because the original story (the embezzler, the Zemo pawn) already cast him that way once, and he is in permanent flight from the casting.

---

## 0.4 The Core Wound

The presenting wound is the embezzlement and prison sentence. That is the surface trauma he cites and that observers cite.

It is not the wound.

The **core wound** is older and structural: **he was loved for what he could produce, by a father who measured, in a household where a brother already occupied the role of the dominant son.** He entered adulthood with no felt experience of being valued for existing. The embezzlement is a *symptom* of the wound — a man who could not bear to be revealed as inadequate, because inadequacy in his developmental schema is not a setback but an erasure. If he is not producing (running the firm, beating Stark, earning his father's posthumous nod), he is not real.

Every subsequent insult tracks this wound with surgical precision:

- **Zemo offers him the chance to be powerful and feared** — i.e., to *produce* dominance — and he takes it. The wound makes him a recruitable asset.
- **Death and resurrection** strip him of the one thing the ionic transformation gave him: the certainty of his own substantiality. He had finally been made "real" (powerful, invulnerable, visible), and then it was revealed that the realness was fragile. Death-phobia is the wound presenting somatically: *I might not be real after all.*
- **The Vision-template** is the cruelest possible iteration of the wound: a *better version of him* — calmer, more loved, more useful, married to the woman he wants — was made *from* him. He is the rough draft of a man other people preferred. He has never recovered from this and probably cannot.
- **The Reaper's hatred** restages the original sibling humiliation in operatic form: the older brother who once outshone him now wishes him erased, and frames the wish as a moral judgment ("you are a soulless thing"). Simon cannot kill him because doing so would confirm the judgment.
- **The Hollywood career** is the wound running an entire industry: he has found a context in which being looked at, applauded, and paid is the daily reward structure. It is the closest thing to a treatment plan he has ever stumbled into. It does not heal, but it palliates.
- **The Revengers** is the wound reversed: rather than being judged inadequate by the Avengers, he preemptively judges *them* inadequate. The defense is novel; the etiology is the same.

---

## 0.5 How The Wound Is Maintained

Wounds of this type persist because the bearer's defenses keep regenerating the conditions that produced them. Simon's maintenance loop is unusually legible:

1. **He selects environments that grade him.** Heroic teams, romantic partnerships with women who have other options, Hollywood, ideological movements. Every chosen context is one in which his standing is evaluated, ranked, and revisable. He does not select unconditional environments (long solitude, contemplative practice, unranked craft) and would not tolerate them long.
2. **He performs to the grader, then resents the grade.** The bravado, the costume choices, the public statements — all are pitched to the current evaluator. When the grade comes back as anything less than "central, indispensable, beloved," he reads it as the original verdict (*not enough*) and either escalates performance or detonates the relationship.
3. **He attaches to a stabilizing other and then over-loads the attachment.** Wanda is the clearest case. He cannot let her be merely a partner; she has to be the mirror that confirms he exists. No partner can bear that weight, and when she cannot, he reads it as withdrawal of regard, and destabilizes.
4. **He somatizes the destabilization.** The ionic flares, the discorporations, the form-instability are not metaphor — his body literally cannot hold shape under sufficient psychological load, because the body is energy and the energy is organized by the self-concept. The somatization then becomes its own trauma, reinforcing death-phobia, reinforcing the wound's premise that he is not solidly real.
5. **He revises at the wire, accruing moral credit that he then under-spends.** The conscience-driven reversals (turning on Zemo, returning to the Avengers, sparing the Reaper, dying for the team) generate genuine moral capital. He does not internalize it. The next stressor arrives and the wound's verdict ("not enough, not real") is still in force. He never gets to keep the credit.
6. **He cannot metabolize the Vision episode.** The one major moral failure — refusing to donate his brain patterns — sits in his interior unmetabolized because metabolizing it would require him to accept that *he, Simon, on his own*, chose jealousy over decency, with no Zemo to blame, no death to excuse it. He prefers to leave it in the shadow. The shadow grows.

The loop is self-perpetuating and has been running for the duration of his recorded existence. It is not getting better. It is not getting catastrophically worse. It is grinding.

---

## 0.6 Operational Summary

**Identity:** Single legal/civilian identity (Simon Williams) co-extensive with the costumed alias. No double-life infrastructure of the Parker/Murdock type — he is publicly known, publicly photographed, IMDB-listed, tax-filed.

**Powers, current best estimate:** Class-100 strength, flight, near-total invulnerability to conventional weaponry, indefinite lifespan, no biological maintenance requirements (food/water/sleep/oxygen all non-essential, though he simulates them for social and psychological reasons — see somatic-pattern subfile). Ionic discharge capability, variable. Form-coherence: degraded, with episodic dispersion and reconstitution under emotional load.

**Mortality status:** Functionally immortal in the operational sense; psychologically convinced he is not. Has died at minimum three times by canonical reckoning and returned each time with reduced form-stability.

**Affiliations, current and dormant:** Avengers (core, recurrent); West Coast Avengers (defunct, formative); Force Works (defunct); Revengers (defunct, his founding, abandoned); Uncanny Avengers (recurrent). Hollywood industry affiliations active and continuous since the late 1980s. SAG-equivalent membership presumed maintained.

**Primary relational dependencies:** Wanda Maximoff (anchor object, intermittent contact, organizing variable). Henry Pym (loyalty axis, mutual). The Vision (rival/twin, unresolved). Eric Williams / Grim Reaper (deceased, unresolved). Hawkeye, Beast, and a rotating cast of Avengers (collegial). No durable parent figure since Sanford Williams' death.

**Behavioral predictability:** High in stable periods, low in destabilized ones. Triggering vectors are well-mapped (see Pressure Points subfile): the embezzlement reference, the Stark/Vision/Reaper comparison set, Wanda's status, and questions about ontological continuity. Any actor with access to two of these four can move him.

**Recruitability:** Non-zero by hostile actors offering a compelling self-narrative — this is the Zemo template and the lesson is permanent. He is not for sale and cannot be bribed in the conventional sense, but he can be **storied** into action against his own interests, as the Revengers period demonstrated when he storied *himself* into it. The defense against this is durable mirror-provision by a trusted source.

**Lethality posture:** Reluctant. Has maintained an effective no-kill discipline across decades and provocations including the brother who hunted him. Will use lethal force against non-sapient/clearly-evil targets, hesitates against humans, hesitates more against persons known to him, will not finish his brother.

**Sobriety:** Continuous. No substance involvement of record. Notable in his industry milieu.

**Recovery profile after destabilization:** Unusually good once a stable mirror is restored. Apologizes in substance. Re-attaches. Does not hold grudges of the durable kind (the Vision being the only partial exception, and even there the resentment is interleaved with guilt, longing, and identification).

**Public exposure:** Maximal. Face on screens, name in trades, paparazzi-tracked. Operational security in the espionage sense: nil. He has chosen this and would not function in shadow.

**Single-sentence operator's read:** *A high-yield, high-loyalty, low-coherence asset whose moral floor is the most reliable thing about him and whose self is the least; handle through trusted mirrors, watch for narrative capture, never reference the embezzlement, never compare him to the Vision, and never bet against him in the last five minutes of a fight he has already decided is the one where he proves he is not the villain.*

---

## 0.7 The Synthesis Sentence (for indexing)

Simon Williams is a man whose body was given to him by an enemy, whose mind was copied for a rival, whose love was always partly someone else's, and whose worth is always being graded — and who, when the grading has gone against him long enough, will still, at the last possible moment and at any cost to himself, choose not to be what they made him. That choice is the man. Everything else is the performance of the man, and the performance, for reasons traceable to a Paterson living room half a century ago, is louder than it needs to be.


## Chronology I — Origin & Childhood

# CHRONOLOGY I — ORIGIN & CHILDHOOD

*Subject: Williams, Simon (no middle name of record; "Simon" appears on all subsequent identification, school enrollment, and indictment papers without initial). File covers the period birth through approximately age 18 — i.e., from natal record to departure from the family home in Paterson for undergraduate enrollment. Canon sources: principal continuity established in* Avengers *#9 (Lee/Heck, 1964); expanded retroactively in* Avengers *#102 (Thomas, 1972, Grim Reaper origin material),* Vision and the Scarlet Witch *vol. 2 #1–6 (Englehart/Leonardi, 1985–86, the Williams family Christmas/funeral sequences),* West Coast Avengers *#2 and the* Wonder Man *limited series (Gerber, 1986; subsequent ongoing, Nicieza, 1991–94), with later texture in the Bendis-era* New Avengers *backups and the 2007* Wonder Man: My Fair Super Hero *and 2023 Williams ongoing.*

---

## 1. BIRTH AND NATAL CIRCUMSTANCES

Simon Williams was born in Paterson, New Jersey, the second son of Sanford Williams and Martha Williams (née surname not given in canon). Birth records as referenced in *Vision and the Scarlet Witch* vol. 2 #2 place him in the family's Eastside Paterson home, not a hospital — the Williamses by this period had house staff and a private obstetrician, a habit Sanford carried over from his own father's preference. Eric Williams, the elder son, was approximately four to six years older (the age gap is implied rather than fixed; *Avengers* #102 establishes Eric as already physically dominant at every documented sibling interaction).

Sanford Williams was the principal of **Williams Innovations**, a Paterson-based industrial-research and light-manufacturing concern in the Edison-revival mold — precision instruments, early electronics, defense-adjacent subcontracting. The firm dates, per offhand reference in *Wonder Man* (1991) #1, to Sanford's own father; Sanford inherited it and was, by Simon's childhood, a regional industrialist of meaningful but second-tier wealth. The family was not in the Stark or Stane class but were comfortably the wealthiest family on their block and probably in their parish.

Martha Williams is the soft spot in the documentary record — she appears explicitly only twice in canon (*Vision and the Scarlet Witch* vol. 2 #2 funeral flashback; brief mention in *Wonder Man* vol. 2 #16) but her absence-in-presence is itself the datum. She predeceases Sanford by several years; Simon is the son who tended her during her illness, a fact Eric throws at him as evidence of his softness in *Avengers* #107.

## 2. FAMILY OF ORIGIN — STRUCTURE AND ATMOSPHERE

**Sanford Williams** is the gravitational center of the household. The man's documented personality, reconstructed from Simon's recollections across roughly forty years of canon, is consistent: a self-made-feeling inheritor (he received the company but believed he earned it), a believer in masculine sufficiency, intolerant of weakness, fluent in shaming-as-pedagogy. He held court at dinner. He compared his sons to each other openly. He framed affection as something earned by performance — a grade, a tackle, a finished chore, a fact recited correctly. In *Vision and the Scarlet Witch* vol. 2 #2 Simon recalls his father by way of a Christmas memory in which Sanford makes a joke at Simon's expense and Eric laughs — the formative tableau, repeated across the canon: father issues the wound, brother amplifies it.

Sanford's parenting style produced two diametric adaptations. Eric internalized the father's idiom and became its sharper edition — physically dominant, contemptuous, predatory. Simon internalized the father's *judgment* and became its target — eager to please, anxiously achieving, structurally insufficient. This is the originating template of the entire adult personality: Simon does not have a problem with authority, he has a problem with *being adequate to* authority. He has never, in canon, defied a father-figure on first contact — he agrees, complies, attempts to satisfy, and only later (Zemo in *Avengers* #9; the Avengers themselves much later) revolts.

**Martha Williams** is positioned in Simon's mental cosmology as the only unconditional regard he ever received. The 1985 *Vision and the Scarlet Witch* sequence has Simon visit the cemetery and address her, not his father — an early datum on which parent he is still in conversation with. Her early death (cause not specified in canon; the illness is depicted as protracted, suggesting cancer or a degenerative condition consistent with mid-twentieth-century chronicity) is the first death Simon witnesses, and the prototype of the death-terror that consumes him post-resurrection. He was, on the evidence, the family member most present at her bedside — a role Eric refused and Sanford emotionally absented from. By the time Simon is in his late teens, the mother is gone; the household consists of Sanford, Eric, and Simon, with predictable consequences.

**Eric Williams** requires a section to himself, which the next chronology entry will expand. For the childhood frame: Eric was the dominant body, the dominant voice, the older boy who established what masculinity in the Williams house *looked like* before Simon had vocabulary to dissent. The cruelty was not occasional. *Avengers* #102 and the retrospective in *Avengers* #352 indicate a pattern of physical hazing — wrestling that went past play, "lessons" that left bruises, public humiliations Sanford treated as character-building. Eric was the *enforcer* of Sanford's worldview, which means Simon grew up in a house with two fathers, one of them his own age peer minus four years, and both of them hostile to the temperament he actually possessed.

## 3. THE EASTSIDE PATERSON ENVIRONMENT

The childhood was not Manhattan-wealthy and not blue-collar-Jersey; it was the specific, identifiable upper-bourgeois Paterson of the postwar industrial families — the kind of household with a yard, a garage Sanford used as a tinkering shop, a Cadillac, a piano nobody played, and a regular cleaning woman whose name Simon learned and Eric did not. The neighborhood produced one identifiable peer cohort in the canon: the kids Simon went to elementary and then to a private day school with, the kind of small set in which one's father's failures and successes were public knowledge. When Williams Innovations later collapses under Simon's management, the humiliation lands on a community that has known him since age six. This is a key datum for understanding the embezzlement panic — Simon was not failing in front of strangers, he was failing in front of the people who'd watched his father preside.

Paterson itself, in the 1950s/60s of his childhood, was a city in industrial decline — the silk mills already gone or going, the small-manufacturing base eroding. Williams Innovations was a partial counter-trend, which means Sanford's identity was built on being a *holdout* against decline, and his sons were the inheritors of a position he considered embattled. The pressure to *not lose what we have* permeates the household. Simon absorbs it as the unbearable possibility of letting his father down. Eric absorbs it as license — if everything is under threat, force is justified.

## 4. THE EARLIEST BEHAVIORAL PATTERNS

A surveillance file would have the elementary school teachers' reports. The behavioral substrate inferable from canon, retroactively projected from documented adult patterns and explicit childhood flashbacks:

- **Eagerness to please adults, especially male authority.** Simon is the boy who answers in class, who volunteers, who brings the apple. The teachers like him; this is part of why Eric resents him. The dynamic is durable into adulthood: Simon will be the Avenger who responds first to Cap's question, who tries hardest in the training room, who courts approval from the senior figure.

- **Performance as relational currency.** From early on Simon learns that *demonstrated* worth is the only worth that registers in the Williams household. He is the boy who shows the report card, recites the multiplication tables, plays the school-pageant lead. This becomes, in adulthood, the Hollywood career — but it is the same circuit, lit at age seven.

- **Conflict avoidance with the brother, conflict avoidance with the father, conflict-seeking with himself.** Simon does not fight Eric back. He does not contradict Sanford. He turns the aggression inward and rehearses his own inadequacy. The adult signature — taking on guilt that is not properly his, apologizing in advance of accusation — is the elaborated form of this childhood script.

- **An imaginative interior.** Simon was the reader, the daydreamer, the boy who narrativized his life into something dignified. He thinks in stories as an adult because he was raised in a house where the external story was unflattering and the only available dignity was an interior one. The "I am the hero of my redemption" frame that organizes his post-Avengers life is the grown form of a child sitting in his bedroom telling himself he is more than what the dinner table said he was.

- **Physical timidity disproportionate to actual capability.** Simon was, by every indication, a normally-built and reasonably coordinated boy; what he lacked was the *willingness* to use his body against another, because the household's chief practitioner of physical aggression was Eric and the household's chief licenser of it was Sanford. Simon's adult under-utilization of his Class-100 strength — the chronic *holding back* that teammates remark on across runs — has a clean childhood origin: he learned that being physically dominant was *Eric's* identity, and to take it on himself would be to become his brother. The hesitation in his fights is a moral hesitation rooted in a sibling boundary drawn at age nine.

- **Attachment to the suffering and the overlooked.** The later loyalty to Hank Pym during the *Avengers* #213 / *West Coast Avengers* #1 disgrace period is anticipated by the childhood pattern of attending to his mother during her decline, and by inferable kindnesses to peripheral household figures (the cleaning woman, Eric's discarded friends). Simon learned in childhood that the position of *the one nobody else will sit with* was a position he understood from the inside.

- **Shame-prone, not guilt-prone, in the strict developmental sense.** Shame is "I am bad"; guilt is "I did a bad thing." Sanford's parenting produced a shame-organized son. Simon's adult collapses (the embezzlement, the Vision refusal, the Revengers turn) are followed by *shame* presentations — withdrawal, theatrical self-condemnation, grand-gesture compensations — rather than by clean guilt-and-repair. The pattern was set before puberty.

## 5. FORMATIVE TRAUMAS — A CATALOGUE

In ascending order of inferable weight:

1. **Chronic comparative belittlement at the family dinner table.** Daily-to-weekly, across roughly fifteen years. The cumulative dose is the principal traumatic exposure of Simon's life — larger in effect than the embezzlement, the death, or the Vision affair, because it laid the substrate that made each subsequent event break him in the specific way it did. *Avengers* #9's monologue — Simon's prison-cell rehearsal of his failures — is recognizably the internalized voice of Sanford Williams.

2. **Eric's hazing.** Documented in *Avengers* #102 retrospective and *West Coast Avengers* #2. The specific incidents canon names are sparse, but the pattern is consistent: physical dominance disguised as play, public humiliations performed for Sanford's amusement, the conversion of younger-brotherhood into a structural subordinate position. The adult Grim Reaper material requires this as backstory — Eric did not become a scythe-murderer from nowhere, and Simon did not become unable to kill his brother from nowhere either. The unkillability of Eric, in Simon's hands, is the unkillability of the childhood bond.

3. **Mother's illness and death.** Date uncertain; Simon was, on the evidence, somewhere between later childhood and mid-adolescence. The trauma has three components: (a) the loss itself, (b) the witness to dying that becomes the template for his own death-terror, and (c) the recognition that neither Sanford nor Eric would hold the vigil — that the family's emotional labor fell to him and was then unrecognized when it was over. Simon's adult pattern of being the man in the room who *stays with* the broken is forged here.

4. **The Stark Industries shadow.** Even in childhood — Howard Stark's firm was already, by the late 1950s/early 1960s, the industrial colossus against which a Paterson firm like Williams Innovations measured itself and lost. Sanford would have spoken Stark's name with the particular bitterness of a man who knows he is being out-competed. Simon grew up hearing *Stark* as the name of the thing his family could not match. The adult resentment of Tony Stark, the chronic comparison-injury, is downstream of the dinner-table economics of his actual boyhood.

5. **The absence of a corrective adult outside the home.** No canon priest, coach, uncle, or teacher emerges as the *other* voice that might have offered Simon a different account of himself. Whatever such figures existed, they did not stick. The Williams household was, for Simon, an unbroken acoustic — every echo confirmed the original verdict. The adult susceptibility to charismatic reframers (Zemo, eventually his own grievance-ideologies) is a function of having no childhood-installed counter-voice.

## 6. THE EARLIEST FORMATIVE BONDS — A LEDGER

- **Martha Williams (mother):** the only unconditional bond. Her death removes the single regulating presence in his life and is the prototype of every subsequent loss of a stabilizing female regard (most consequentially Wanda). Simon's adult need-for-a-mirror has a name, and the name is his mother's.

- **Sanford Williams (father):** the principal organizing object. Simon's psyche is shaped *against* Sanford's verdict — every adult achievement is a posthumous argument with him. Sanford's death (offstage; referenced as preceding the *Avengers* #9 inheritance) does not free Simon; it fixes the verdict permanently, because the judge can no longer revise the ruling.

- **Eric Williams (brother):** the primary peer relationship of childhood and the structural antagonist of the entire adult life. The bond is unbreakable in the precise sense that *enmeshment* is unbreakable — Simon cannot let Eric go because Eric is constitutive of who Simon is. The Grim Reaper arc is the externalization of a sibling tie Simon never resolved at the dinner table.

- **No documented childhood friends of consequence.** The canon is silent on a best-friend figure, a first-love figure, a confidant. The silence is itself the evidence — Simon was a boy whose primary relationships were all inside the household, which is to say he had no relational counterweight to what the household was doing to him. The adult pattern of forming intense, identity-organizing attachments to team-figures (the Avengers as a *replacement family*, Wanda as a *replacement maternal-romantic mirror*, Pym as a *replacement broken-brother*) reflects a man who never developed the lateral relational skills a different childhood would have given him.

- **An inferable cohort of school peers** who knew the Williams family and would, decades later, read about Simon's embezzlement in the Paterson paper. This invisible audience is part of why the original failure was unsurvivable in his interior life — he had been *watched* by these people his entire childhood, and the watching never stopped.

## 7. EARLIEST SOMATIC AND BEHAVIORAL TELLS, INFERABLE FROM ADULT PATTERNS

A total-surveillance file projects backward as well as forward. The following micro-patterns are visible in Simon's adult body and are of a kind that establish in childhood:

- **A reflexive glance toward the senior male in the room before speaking** — adult Simon does this with Cap, with Pym in the early days, with directors on set. Origin: checking Sanford's face for permission, age four through eighteen.

- **A tendency to laugh at his own expense pre-emptively** — disarms the room before the room disarms him. Origin: getting the joke in before Eric did.

- **Vocal projection that runs slightly louder than required** — the adult performer's voice. Origin: a boy who learned that not being heard meant not being counted, in a household where Eric was always the louder one.

- **A particular stillness when criticized** — the freeze that recurs in the post-resurrection arc has a developmental antecedent: the boy at the table who has learned not to flinch visibly because flinching invites more. The combat-freeze of *Avengers* #160s is the same body remembering an older lesson.

- **Compulsive narrativizing of his own circumstances** — the internal monologue that consumes panels of his adult solo books, the running self-commentary, the *here is what this means about me* reflex. Origin: a boy who had to construct, in his own head, a dignified account of a life that the people around him would not dignify.

- **Difficulty receiving plain praise** — adult Simon deflects, jokes, or amplifies; he does not simply absorb. Origin: praise was, in his childhood, either absent or conditional or weaponized (Eric praised to make Simon's failure visible). He has no childhood schema for praise as a gift.

## 8. THE GROUND-FLOOR SYNTHESIS

By the time Simon Williams leaves the Paterson house for college — the canon implies a business-school track, eventual return to manage Williams Innovations after Sanford's death — the personality structure that will carry him through every subsequent catastrophe is already installed. He is a shame-organized, performance-dependent, conflict-avoidant, narrativizing young man with one dead mirror (his mother), one judging ghost (his father), one inescapable rival (his brother), one industrial nemesis his family has named for him (Stark), and no lateral relational ballast.

Every later event in the file — the embezzlement, the Zemo recruitment, the conscience-revolt, the death-terror, the Vision-template wound, the Wanda dependency, the discorporated lover phase, the Revengers turn — is a permutation of conditions that were laid down in a single house in Eastside Paterson before he turned eighteen. The man who, in *Avengers* #9, sits in a prison cell rehearsing his own inadequacy in language indistinguishable from his father's is not a man recently broken. He is a man who has been preparing this monologue since he was old enough to set the table.

Simon Williams did not become Wonder Man because Baron Zemo found him. Baron Zemo found him because he had already, by every internal measure, been waiting to be found by *someone* who would offer him an account of himself he could believe in. The ionic rays merely lit up what the dinner table had already wired.


## Chronology II — Formation & Emergence

# CHRONOLOGY II — FORMATION & EMERGENCE

*Subject: WILLIAMS, Simon — file segment covering approx. ages 14 through 27, terminating at the first death and the closure of the formative cycle. Source base: Avengers #9 (1964) and its retroactive expansions through Marvel Premiere #55 (Gruenwald, 1980), the Wonder Man one-shot/origin retellings of the 1980s, Vision and the Scarlet Witch maxi (Englehart, 1985–86) for sibling and childhood material, West Coast Avengers backstory issues, and the Wonder Man ongoing (Jones, 1991–94) for re-narrated youth. Where canon offers competing accounts, both are logged; the file does not select one.*

---

## I. THE PATERSON HOUSEHOLD, c. AGES 14–18

The Williams residence in Paterson, New Jersey is the first surveillable environment: a three-story house on the wealthier slope of the city, a half-mile from the Williams Innovations plant on the river, with grounds, a detached garage, and a paneled study that is the father's province and the older son's training ground. Sanford Williams is a self-made industrialist of the first postwar generation, a man whose authority is enacted at the dinner table and at the plant; his pedagogy with his sons is the standard pedagogy of his cohort — comparison, withholding, performance review at age thirteen. Eric, the older son by roughly four years, is the recipient of Sanford's attention because Eric is the recipient of Sanford's temperament: physical, combative, undeferred, gifted with the kind of cold practical intelligence that recognizes leverage.

Simon is the second child and the disappointment of contrast. He is taller than his peers by adolescence, broad-shouldered, with the soft handsomeness that reads as weakness in a household that prizes hardness. The mother — sparsely rendered in canon, dead before the criminal trial — is the only adult who treats him as if he is enough; her death (cancer, mid-teens in most accountings) removes the single buffer between Simon and the comparative regime. After her funeral the household contracts to three men, and the three-man dynamic is fixed for the remainder of Sanford's life: father and elder son in alliance, Simon adjacent, performing competence that is never quite credited.

Behavioral signatures from this period, derivable from later self-report and Eric's mocking recollections in Vision and the Scarlet Witch:

- Simon retreats into reading — adventure fiction, pulp serials, early science fiction, and the matinee culture of the 1950s. He is the child who memorizes the dialogue. The future Hollywood career is prefigured here: the boy who knew which line came next.
- He develops the placating smile that will appear in every team photograph of his adulthood — the half-grin that is meant to communicate that he is not a threat, that he is on your side, that there is no need to correct him.
- Athletic competence is real but unspectacular; he plays football in high school, runs the middle distance in track, never captains. Eric, by report, is the school's wrestling standout and a minor local legend. Simon is "Eric Williams's brother" to every coach in the county.
- Earliest documentable defense pattern: when reprimanded, Simon agrees first and processes later. He does not argue with Sanford. The grievances are deposited internally, where they accrete into the deferred resentment that will manifest, decades later, as the Revengers manifesto.

The relationship with Eric in this period is more complicated than mere rivalry. Eric is also, intermittently, Simon's protector — beating up the local boys who mock Simon, intervening when Sanford's discipline crosses into sustained verbal cruelty, including Simon in older-boy outings on terms calibrated to make Simon feel chosen. The pattern produces the lifelong impossibility: Simon cannot hate Eric cleanly because Eric was, in childhood, the only person who saw him being mistreated and occasionally stepped in. This is the structural reason the Grim Reaper recurs across four decades. The file should not read it as sentimentality. It is a debt that was incurred before Simon could refuse it.

## II. UNIVERSITY AND THE PROFESSIONAL APPRENTICESHIP, c. AGES 18–22

Simon attends college in the Northeast — variously rendered as a small private institution and, in later retellings, as a state school where he studies business administration and electrical engineering on the assumption that he will inherit the company. His academic record is upper-middle: bright enough to grasp the material, not original enough to extend it. The professors who later recall him to Avengers magazine reporters (a 1970s in-universe profile referenced in Marvel Premiere #55) describe a polite, slightly performative student who participated in seminars by restating others' arguments more eloquently. The pattern of borrowed identity is already operational: Simon is the student who makes other people's ideas sound better.

Romantically, the college period is sparse. Two relationships are alluded to in later dialogue, both with women who broke it off; one, named in passing in the Jones Wonder Man series, was a theater student, which is one of the earliest documentable instances of Simon being drawn toward performers. The pattern recurs with Wanda (a performer of magic), with the Hollywood actresses of the West Coast era, and with the audience-as-lover that the screen career later supplies.

After graduation he enters Williams Innovations at a junior engineering position. Sanford's pedagogy here is harsher than in adolescence because the stakes are public: Simon is observed by employees who report to Sanford, and his every error is debriefed. Eric, by this point, has refused the company entirely and pursued the criminal trajectory that will eventuate in the Maggia and the Reaper identity; Sanford's disappointment in Eric is folded into disappointment in Simon, who is now the sole heir to a succession Sanford never wanted to plan. Simon receives, in his early twenties, both the inheritance he wanted and the certainty that he received it only because his brother was unavailable.

## III. THE INHERITANCE AND THE FAILURE, c. AGES 22–25

Sanford Williams dies — heart attack in most accountings, in his office, at his desk, the patriarchal cliché completed. Simon is twenty-two or twenty-three. He inherits the company in a market that has already begun to be reorganized by Stark Industries, whose military contracts, miniaturization patents, and post-Iron Man cachet are draining the customer base for mid-tier electronics firms in the corridor from Paterson to Trenton.

The granular failure pattern, reconstructed from the trial transcripts referenced in Avengers #9's flashback and the more detailed sequence in Marvel Premiere #55:

- Simon does not lay off staff aggressively enough. He has known these people since he was a child visiting the plant; the personnel decisions that the company's survival would have required, he cannot execute. This is the moral floor showing up as commercial incompetence — the same loyalty to broken people that will later mark his Avengers career.
- He pursues product lines that Sanford had been developing — passively, by inertia, because re-pivoting feels like a repudiation of his father. The pivot is what the market needs.
- He hires consultants whose advice he then half-takes, optimizing for the appearance of decisive leadership rather than the substance.
- He attempts a meeting with Tony Stark in this period (referenced in a Busiek-era flashback, *Iron Man / Wonder Man* exchange) at which he hopes to negotiate a subcontracting arrangement. Stark is cordial and condescending; the meeting produces nothing. Simon files Stark, that day, as the third comparative figure after Sanford and Eric.

By the second year of his tenure the company is insolvent in fact and presented as solvent in books. Simon embezzles — the method is conventional, falsified inventory and shell receivables, the kind of fraud a man with engineering training and no criminal sophistication commits because it looks plausible from inside. He is caught by an auditor. The arrest is reported in the local press; the trial is brief; the sentence is custodial, not suspended, because the prosecutor wants an example and Sanford Williams's name carries enough civic memory in Passaic County to make Simon's name useful for that purpose.

The granular humiliation events of this period — the file flags each as a permanent psychological landmark:

1. The walk from the courthouse to the transport vehicle, photographed by a Paterson newspaper. Simon is wearing a suit that is now too loose because he has lost weight on remand. The photograph appears in Sunday papers across the New York metropolitan area.
2. The funeral of the company — the auction of the Williams Innovations plant, which Simon does not attend because he is incarcerated, and which Eric does attend, reportedly, and reportedly buys nothing.
3. The first week in the state correctional facility, during which Simon, who has never been in a fight he initiated, is forced to learn the carceral protocols of deference and posture. He emerges from this week with the lifelong somatic tell of slight shoulder-rolling when entering an unfamiliar room — a residual scan for threat that observers in the Avengers Mansion era will read as actorly affect and that is in fact a prison habit.
4. The first refused visit from Eric. Eric comes, Simon refuses, and Eric leaves a message through a guard that the file logs verbatim in one West Coast Avengers flashback: a single sentence to the effect that Simon should have asked for help.

That last item is the seed of the Grim Reaper. Eric's grievance is not, primarily, that Simon turned to crime; it is that Simon turned to crime *without consulting his criminal brother*, who would have done it more competently. The pride wound, in Eric, is enormous and is never repaired.

## IV. THE ZEMO RECRUITMENT, c. AGE 25–26

Heinrich Zemo — the original Baron, in this period operating out of a Latin American compound and assembling the first iteration of the Masters of Evil — identifies Simon through a network of informants tracking high-profile white-collar prisoners with technical training and grievance against either Stark or the Avengers. Simon's file lands on Zemo's desk because it satisfies three predicates: (a) media-recognizable name, useful for the Trojan-horse operation, (b) engineering competence that makes the ionic process survivable in theory, and (c) a documentable resentment of Stark, which Zemo can leverage rhetorically without having to manufacture motivation.

The approach is made through an intermediary — a lawyer, in the original Avengers #9 telling — who visits Simon in the third or fourth month of his sentence with a proposition framed as an appeal opportunity. The exfiltration is clean: Simon is moved at night, transported to Zemo's facility, presented with the choice. The choice is structured to produce only one answer. Simon will receive power, freedom, the destruction of Stark by proxy, and the restoration of public standing as a hero; the alternative is the remainder of his sentence and the post-release impossibility of returning to any field that knew his name.

Behavioral observation, derivable from Zemo's later debrief notes referenced in Marvel Premiere #55: Simon asks the right questions but does not press the answers. He wants to be convinced. The deal is closed within hours. This is the first instance of the lifelong pattern in which Simon accepts a narrative offered by a charismatic reframer because the narrative offers him a self he can wear. Zemo gives him a story (the wronged man returns as a hero), a name (Wonder Man — the gauche grandiosity is Zemo's, not Simon's; Simon accepts it without modification), a costume (the red and green, designed for maximum visual identification), and a body.

The procedure itself, the ionic ray treatment, is conducted across several days. The file notes the following granular details derivable from Avengers #9 and the expanded sequences of the 1980s:

- Simon is conscious for portions of the procedure. The pain is described, in his own later recollection to Wanda in Vision and the Scarlet Witch #4, as the sensation of being "unspooled and rewound." The metaphor is psychologically load-bearing: he experiences the transformation as the dissolution of one self and the imposition of another, which is the truth of it.
- His physical mass increases; his hair color shifts subtly; his eyes become the photographic red that becomes the visual signature.
- He emerges able to lift, on the first test, a small armored vehicle. He laughs. The laugh is recorded; it is the first instance of the bravado-laugh that will become a lifelong affect regulator.
- Zemo informs him, at the end of the second week, of two facts that will define the rest of his life. First, the process is unstable; without further treatments, which only Zemo can administer, Simon will die within weeks. Second, the price of further treatments is the destruction of the Avengers from within. The leash is metallurgical.

The dependency structure here is exact: Simon's new self is owned by the man who manufactured it. This is the template for every subsequent relationship in which Simon's coherence is held by another party — the Avengers as restorers of his moral self, Wanda as the unconscious summoner of his discorporated form, the audience as the validator of his Hollywood self. Zemo did not invent the pattern; he installed it as a physical fact.

## V. THE INFILTRATION AND THE TURN, AVENGERS #9

Simon presents himself to the Avengers, in the original sequence, at their Fifth Avenue mansion. The cover story is engineered to elicit protective response: he is the victim of an ionic accident, dying, in need of help. The Avengers — Captain America, Thor, Iron Man, Giant-Man, the Wasp — accept him in a meeting that lasts under an hour. Iron Man, who knows the Williams name from the Stark Industries competitive file, vouches for the legitimacy of the background and is the most affirmatively welcoming. The granular irony is logged: Stark, the comparative figure Simon has resented for years, is the Avenger who most warmly extends membership. Simon does not at this moment recognize the irony; he will recognize it later, repeatedly, in every subsequent encounter with Stark.

The infiltration proceeds across approximately seventy-two hours of Avengers Mansion residency. Behavioral observations:

- Simon eats with the team, sleeps in a guest room, is given a tour of the equipment.
- He is observed by the Wasp to be "jumpy" and by Giant-Man to be "thoughtful in a worried way." These are the first external readings of the conscience that has begun to operate.
- He engages Captain America in a corridor conversation about heroism and second chances — the conversation is logged in flashback in Captain America #321 — which Cap conducts with the seriousness he reserves for moral questions and which lodges in Simon as the first authoritative voice that has ever taken his moral self at face value.
- The combat operation is launched; the Avengers are lured into Zemo's trap; Simon is positioned to execute the betrayal.

The turn occurs at the operational moment. The granular sequence: Simon has the team incapacitated and the trigger in hand; he is required to complete the act; he does not. He turns, attacks Zemo's apparatus, frees the team, and absorbs the lethal feedback. The reasoning he provides aloud, in the panel — that he cannot be the villain in the story — is the first articulation of the self-narrative that will organize the rest of his life. He could not bear to be the villain in his own story. This is the formative *positive* identification, the counterweight to the formative humiliations. Whatever else Simon Williams will become, the first significant choice he made as a powered being was to refuse the role assigned by the man who made him.

The cost is the unstable physiology Zemo warned of. Simon collapses, enters the coma-state that will be read as death for the better part of a decade of publication time, and is laid out in a Mansion chamber that becomes, for the next several years of Avengers continuity, the de facto crypt of the team's first martyr. The Avengers do not bury him; Iron Man, specifically, refuses to declare him dead, citing the unfamiliarity of ionic physiology. This refusal — which Simon will not learn about until after resurrection — is the second granular irony: Stark, the comparative figure, is the one who maintains the possibility of Simon's return.

## VI. FALLOUT, AGES 26–27 (THE DORMANT PERIOD)

The dormant period is not interior to Simon — he is not conscious — but it is formative because the world in which he will wake has been shaped by his absence. The file logs the relevant exterior developments:

- Eric Williams, on learning of his brother's "death" via the Avengers' public statement, completes the trajectory toward criminal-enhanced identity. He acquires the scythe-weapon and the Grim Reaper costume within two years; his stated grievance is the Avengers' culpability for Simon's death, which is the rationalization that licenses the killing he was already psychologically prepared to do. Simon will spend the next four decades cleaning up after this transition.
- The Vision is constructed (Avengers #57–58) using brain engrams recorded by the Avengers, with Stark's authorization, during Simon's dormancy. Simon is not consulted because he cannot be consulted; the consent question is, in the original construction, moot. The retroactive ethics will become load-bearing in the West Coast era. The Vision wakes with Simon's emotional palette, Simon's capacity for love, Simon's relational template — and forms the relationship with Wanda that Simon, on revival, will discover already in place.
- The Avengers do not, in this period, mythologize Simon. He is referenced occasionally in dialogue as a noble figure; he is not made a banner. This is mercy and oversight in equal measure: he is preserved as a recoverable man rather than fixed as a martyr, and he is also, simply, not the figure the team's iconography requires. He is, as he always was, adjacent.

When Simon wakes — a decade later in publication, an indeterminate but substantial interval in-universe — every relational position that mattered to him will have been filled by someone else. His brother is a mass murderer. His engrams are walking around in a synthezoid married to a witch he has not yet met. His company is dismantled. His father is dead. His city has moved on. The formative cycle closes here: the man who was reconstituted to be a Trojan horse and who refused the assignment is preserved, on ice, while the world in which his refusal mattered rearranges itself without him.

---

**Formation summary, file-level:** the identity that emerges from this period is a layered construction whose every layer is borrowed and whose every layer is structurally unstable. The base layer is the comparative-failure child of the Paterson household. The second layer is the embezzler-prisoner, the publicly humiliated heir. The third layer is the Zemo-manufactured ionic body, leashed at construction to a master who is dead within the first operation. The fourth layer is the Avengers-affirmed conscience, the moral self that briefly cohered around a single refusal. The fifth layer — already forming in his absence — is the externalization of his engrams into a separate being who will live the life he was preparing to live. By age twenty-seven, in subjective time, Simon Williams has been a son, a failure, a criminal, a weapon, a hero, and a corpse. Every subsequent decade of the file will be him attempting to discover which of those was him.


## Chronology III — The Defining Middle Period

# CHRONOLOGY III — THE DEFINING MIDDLE PERIOD

*Subject: WILLIAMS, Simon — file segment covering the long arc from confirmed recovery from death-phobia through the Force Works dissolution and the post-Busiek discorporated period. Approximately the mid-1980s through the turn of the century in publication terms; functionally, the years in which the subject established, exercised, and then catastrophically tested every adult pattern visible in the current file.*

---

## 1. The West Coast Relocation and the Manufactured Adult Life

The middle period opens with Subject's relocation to Los Angeles as a charter member of the West Coast Avengers compound at Palos Verdes (founding roster: Hawkeye, Mockingbird, Iron Man — Rhodes-era — Tigra, and Subject). This is the first time in his recorded existence that Subject has chosen a home rather than inherited or been assigned one. The Paterson, NJ family estate is by this point liquidated; the Williams Innovations property has long since been absorbed by competitors (the file notes no attempt by Subject to repurchase or revisit it across the entire period — an avoidance behavior consistent with the unmetabolized embezzlement shame). The Palos Verdes compound — bunkhouse architecture, communal kitchen, motor pool, hangar — gives him for the first time a daily texture: a room he sleeps in (rarely, given ionic metabolism, but he maintains the room as a stage set of normalcy), a chair at a breakfast table, neighbors who knock.

Within weeks of arrival he establishes the routes that will define the period: a regular drive up Pacific Coast Highway in an open-top vehicle (vehicle changes across the era — convertibles, consistently red, consistently American — but the route is stable); a standing weight-room ritual that is functionally theatrical, since he does not require training, but which he performs at the same hours each morning, often when teammates are present, often shirtless; and a recurring lunch circuit through a small number of Westside delicatessens and Burbank commissaries where he is recognized by name. The pattern is observable: he constructs witnesses. He does not eat alone if he can avoid it. He does not train alone. He does not, in this period, sleep alone for any extended stretch if there is an alternative.

## 2. The Hollywood Career as Sublimation Engine

The acting and stunt-work career, initiated in this period and pursued throughout it, is the single most psychologically productive development of Subject's middle life. The progression is documentable:

- **Stunt-double phase (early West Coast era).** Subject works uncredited as a stunt performer on action features, using the ionic invulnerability to absorb falls, fires, vehicle impacts that would kill or maim a baseline human. Pay is modest; he does not need money (Avengers stipend plus residual Williams trust fragments) but pursues the work compulsively. Reading: he is paying himself to be hit. The masochistic edge is not subtle. He volunteers for gags other stuntmen refuse; he is described by crews as "the guy who doesn't flinch." The flinchlessness is biological but performed as character.
- **Featured-stunt and second-unit phase.** Within roughly eighteen months he is being requested by name. He picks up an agent (the file logs at least three across the period, with a consistent pattern of dismissal-then-rehire cycles correlated to Subject's moods). He acquires a SAG card under his civilian name.
- **Speaking-role and lead phase.** Mid-period, he transitions to credited acting work — supporting roles in action features, B-tier leads in genre pictures, the occasional television guest spot. The work is consistently in the action-adventure register; no comedies, no drama, no theatrical work despite repeated public statements of interest. The avoidance is diagnostic: he will not put down the persona long enough to risk a register in which he might be exposed as not knowing how to be a person.
- **The publicity apparatus.** Subject grants more interviews in this period than any other Avenger except Stark. He poses for magazine covers; he attends premieres; he is photographed at restaurants. The file's longitudinal analysis of these photographs shows a stable grammar: he is almost never photographed alone, almost always with a recognizable second person, and his facial expression in candid versus posed shots diverges sharply — the candid jaw is tighter, the eyes scan the periphery.

The acting career is genuine sublimation in the technical sense. It channels exhibitionism, grandiosity, and the need to be seen into a socially sanctioned and remunerative form. It also gives him a workable answer, for the first time in his life, to the question "what do you do." The answer is no longer "I failed at my father's company" or "I'm an Avenger." It is "I'm an actor." For Subject, whose self-concept is externally referenced, the new label has measurable stabilizing effect across the first half of the period.

The cost: every role he plays is an action hero, and every action hero he plays is a stylized version of himself. He is therefore being paid, repeatedly, to perform the persona he uses to compensate for the absence of a stable interior. This is sublimation that doubles as entrenchment. By the back half of the period he can no longer reliably distinguish his "on" voice from his "off" voice. Teammates note the quipping intensifying; the one-liners begin to land in moments that do not call for them. The performance is colonizing the substrate.

## 3. The Hank Pym Loyalty — The Single Cleanest Relationship in the File

Across the entire middle period, Subject's friendship with Henry Pym is the one relational variable that does not destabilize, does not weaponize itself, does not curdle. The file regards this as the most important positive data point in the entire dossier.

The friendship is forged in Pym's worst stretch — the court-martial, the divorce from van Dyne, the suicidal interval, the abortive Egghead frame-up. Subject's behavior throughout is uncharacteristically quiet. He does not perform support; he provides it. He shows up to the safe house. He sits with Pym for hours without speaking. He testifies. He vouches in front of teammates who are skeptical or angry. There is no public statement, no press cycle, no Wonder Man image management around any of it. The contrast with every other relational pattern in his life is so sharp it functions as a control variable: this is what Subject looks like when his self-regard is not on the line.

The file's read: Pym is the only person in Subject's adult life who is more obviously broken than Subject is. The asymmetry creates psychological safety. Subject does not have to compete with Pym, cannot disappoint Pym in the ways he disappoints Wanda or Stark or his brother, and is not measured by Pym against any standard he cannot meet. With Pym, the underlying decent man — the one who turned on Zemo in the first issue — is allowed to operate without the persona overhead. The friendship persists across decades, across team changes, across Subject's worst behaviors. Pym is named in conversations Subject has when he is genuinely frightened; Pym is the person Subject calls when he does not know what to do. No other figure in the file occupies this slot.

This relationship is the single strongest counter-evidence against any reading of Subject as fundamentally narcissistic. A narcissist of the malignant type does not maintain a multi-decade loyalty to a man whose principal social value is that he is broken.

## 4. The Vision — The Slow Catastrophe

The middle period contains the full arc of Subject's relationship with the synthezoid whose neural template was copied from his own recorded engrams. The arc proceeds in identifiable stages:

**Stage one — uneasy fraternity.** Early West Coast era. Subject and the Vision interact on professional terms; both perform civility. Subject is observed, however, to avoid being in frame with the Vision for photographs, to leave rooms when the Vision enters them under non-mission circumstances, and to refer to the Vision in conversation by title rather than name with notable consistency. The Vision is, structurally, the man Subject would have been if Subject had been allowed to be good. The mirror is intolerable.

**Stage two — open rivalry, displaced.** Mid-period. Subject's interest in Wanda becomes overt enough that teammates remark on it. He flirts in the Vision's presence; he stages competence displays during joint missions; he offers Wanda rides, meals, conversations. The rivalry is conducted through Wanda rather than directly with the Vision, which is the form Subject's male competitions always take — through a third party who confers value.

**Stage three — the dismantling and the refusal.** The defining moral event of the entire middle period and arguably of Subject's life. When the Vision is captured, disassembled, and rebuilt as a blank, the restoration of his personality requires Subject's brain patterns. Subject refuses. The refusal is conducted publicly, with rationales — concerns about identity violation, about consent, about whether a being reassembled from his template is meaningfully the Vision at all — that are not wrong in the abstract but that are, in context, instrumentally jealous. Subject wants Wanda. He withholds the means of restoring her husband. He knows what he is doing while he is doing it. The file logs this as the single most morally compromised choice in Subject's recorded existence and notes that he never recovers from it cleanly. He produces, across subsequent years, three distinct versions of the rationale, each more elaborate than the last. The escalating intellectualization is the tell. He is litigating it to himself, repeatedly, because the original conviction will not hold.

**Stage four — the partial restitution, the unstable Vision, the wreckage.** The Vision is restored in altered, drained form (white, affectless). Wanda's relationship with him collapses. Subject is, in narrow practical terms, the beneficiary. He cannot enjoy it. The middle stretch of the period shows him alternately courting Wanda and avoiding her, sometimes within the same week.

**Stage five — the children, the displacement.** When Wanda's twins are revealed to be functions of her hex-power rather than biologically tenable, the household disintegrates. Subject is present for the aftermath. He does not — to his credit — attempt to capitalize. The file notes a marked increase in his Pym contact during this stretch, suggesting the Pym relationship is functioning as the regulator that keeps him from making the catastrophic move he would otherwise be primed to make.

## 5. The Wanda Romance — Consummation, Brevity, Loss

The relationship Subject has wanted since first sight is, in this period, achieved. Wanda turns to him in the wreckage of the Vision marriage; they are involved romantically for a recordable stretch in the late portion of the period. The file's longitudinal pattern analysis of this stretch is striking: Subject is, by his own metrics, happier than at any other recorded point in his life, and *worse at being an Avenger than at any other recorded point in his life*. His combat performance during the relationship is diminished; he is hesitant, distracted, prone to checking on Wanda mid-engagement, prone to grandstanding when she is watching. The performance-of-self that the Hollywood work had partially absorbed reasserts itself in tactical settings, with measurable cost.

The relationship ends because Wanda elects to attempt to repair the Vision — not, on the record, because she no longer loves Subject, but because the unfinished business of the marriage is unfinished and she is the one who must finish it. Subject does not handle this well. He does not handle it badly either; he handles it by going quiet, increasing his stunt-work intake, accepting riskier gags, and being photographed at premieres with a rotating cast of female companions, none of whom appear twice. The pattern is recognizable as performed-okayness over an unmetabolized grief.

The loss of Wanda in the back half of the period is the proximate cause of every subsequent destabilization in the file. He has had her; he has lost her; he now knows the precise shape of what is missing. The earlier era of unrequited longing was psychologically more tolerable than the post-consummation absence, because longing has a future tense and absence does not.

## 6. The Grim Reaper Subarc — The Brother Who Will Not Stay Dead, Will Not Stay Alive

Across the middle period, Eric Williams returns repeatedly. The file logs no fewer than six distinct re-emergence events across the era, with the cadence accelerating in the back half. The pattern of each is structurally identical: Reaper surfaces, makes a public threat, draws Subject specifically into engagement, monologues a variation on the "you are a soulless thing wearing my brother's face" theme, attempts to kill or convert Subject, is defeated, is left alive, dies in the process or shortly after, returns in some altered form (zombified, ionically warped, magically resurrected, etc.).

The file's reading of why this loop persists for as long as it does in this period: Subject will not let Eric die. Each opportunity to permit a clean death — by withholding rescue, by allowing the fall, by lethal force in self-defense — is declined. Subject talks himself, and teammates, out of finality every time. The reluctance to kill is presented as ethics and is partially that, but the structural function is preservation of the rivalry that has organized him since childhood. Without Eric, Subject loses the brother who consistently rates him as inadequate — and that rating, however painful, is one of the few stable fixed points in his identity. The file reads the Reaper as Subject's externalized superego in costume, and notes that Subject will not destroy his own superego no matter how many times it tries to behead him.

The arc closes, provisionally, near the end of the middle period with Subject finally burying Eric. The burial is performed alone, off-team, in a small ceremony Subject does not invite witnesses to. This is one of the only recorded acts in the entire period that Subject performs without an audience. The file flags it as significant. He grieves the brother privately because he cannot bear for anyone to see him grieving a man who hated him. The shame substrate is doing double work: shame at the brother, shame at the grief.

## 7. The Force Works Era — Leadership and Its Failures

When the West Coast Avengers franchise dissolves (acrimoniously, with Iron Man's faction and Hawkeye's faction parting over governance), Subject is folded into the successor organization, Force Works, alongside Stark, Spider-Woman (Carpenter), Scarlet Witch, and others. He functions in a quasi-leadership capacity — not the formal field commander but the connective tissue of the roster, the morale figure, the public face.

This is the first sustained period in which Subject is given institutional authority. His performance is mixed. The diagnostic findings:

- He is *good* at the public-facing duties. The press handles, the recruiting pitches, the public optics — Subject's persona absorbs these naturally.
- He is *poor* at the dyadic management. Conflicts between teammates are handled by deflection, humor, or absence. He does not arbitrate; he changes the subject.
- He is *unreliable* in the strategic role. He defers to Stark on tactics — the Stark deference is itself a tell, the older inadequacy script intact — and produces few independent operational plans.
- He is *exemplary* in the loss-acceptance role. When the team takes casualties, Subject is the one who speaks at the services, who writes to families, who keeps the dead in conversation.

The era ends, for Subject specifically, in his second death — a deliberate, ionic-overload sacrifice in a confrontation with Kree forces. The death is, by his own internal logic, the completion of an arc: the man who failed to be his father's heir, failed at business, failed at fidelity to Zemo, failed (in his estimation) at the Vision question, and lost Wanda, now succeeds at the one act he has practiced before — dying for the team.

The file's reading of this second death is unsparing: it is not entirely heroism. There is a component of relief in it. He is, at the moment of the sacrifice, more certain that this is the right thing than he has been about any other choice in years. The certainty is suspect. Subject's certainty correlates inversely with his stability.

## 8. The Discorporated Interval — Existence as a Function of Another's Need

For a long stretch after the Force Works death, Subject is dead in the conventional sense — interred, mourned, eulogized (the file notes Pym's eulogy in particular, and Subject's later reaction to learning of its contents). He is, however, not gone. The ionic substrate that constitutes his body persists in dispersed form, and Wanda — herself increasingly probability-altering at this stage — unconsciously begins to summon his presence.

He returns, at first, as a voice. Then as an apparition. Then as an intermittent solid form, capable of action only in her presence and only when she needs him. He cannot leave her side without dissipating. He cannot maintain solid form when she is asleep, when she is distracted, when she is with the Vision (in the Vision's altered form). His existence has become, ontologically, what it always was psychologically: contingent on her regard.

The file regards this stretch as the most diagnostically revealing period in the entire dossier. The metaphor has become the mechanism. Subject is not a man who feels he exists only when seen; he is a man who *literally only exists when seen, by one specific person*. The somatic correlate of the personality structure is total. Every defense, every compensatory persona, every grandiose Hollywood gesture is, in this interval, stripped away — he cannot perform, because performance requires a body, and his body requires her witnessing.

He does not, in this interval, suffer. The file notes this carefully. He is reported as serene, even content. The contentment is also diagnostic: he is happiest when his existence is least his own, because then he cannot fail at it. The absence of agency is the absence of the possibility of inadequacy. For a man whose foundational wound is inadequacy, non-existence-except-as-extension-of-the-beloved is, perversely, a state of grace.

Reconstitution is gradual. Through the late portion of the period he stabilizes, reacquires independent form, reacquires the capacity to leave her presence. He rejoins the Avengers in the Busiek-era reformation. He resumes the Hollywood work, though more sporadically. He resumes, gradually, the persona.

He does not, however, return to the man he was before the second death. The discorporated interval has altered him. He is quieter. He is more prone to standing at the edges of rooms. He is observed, by Pym specifically, to flicker — briefly, almost imperceptibly — at moments of strong emotion. The body does not entirely hold. The file's prognosis at the close of this period correctly anticipates the eventual destabilizations of the subsequent era: a substrate that has been discorporated once is a substrate that has learned how to disperse, and the body, like the self, remembers the lessons it should not have had to learn.

## 9. Pattern Evolution Across the Period — A Synthesis

The middle period takes the patterns evident in Subject's earlier history and tests them against sustained adult conditions. The findings, condensed:

- The need to be seen *intensifies* rather than abates with the achievement of fame. Recognition does not satisfy the underlying hunger; it accustoms the system to higher doses.
- The capacity for sustained loyalty (Pym) is confirmed as a stable feature, not a phase. Where the relational geometry permits it — asymmetric, non-competitive, with a partner whose brokenness is more visible than his own — Subject is capable of decades-long fidelity.
- The capacity for sustained romantic stability is *not* confirmed. The Wanda relationship demonstrates that achievement of the love object collapses the structure that the longing had been holding up.
- The reluctance to kill, including to kill an enemy who repeatedly tries to kill him, is confirmed as a stable feature across the period. This is the moral floor referenced in the synthesis. It does not waver. He will refuse violence even when violence would be operationally and ethically defensible.
- The vulnerability to charismatic reframers is confirmed as a stable feature, but with a meaningful update: the reframer no longer needs to be external. Across this period, Subject begins to demonstrate the ability to charismatic-reframe *to himself* — to construct his own narrative justifications for actions his earlier self would have refused. The Vision refusal is the prototype. The full flowering of this capacity occurs in the subsequent period.
- The body-and-self isomorphism is confirmed and intensified. The discorporated interval makes literal what was figurative; subsequent destabilizations will continue this trajectory.

Subject exits the middle period as a man who has lived a recognizable adult life — career, friendships, a love affair, losses, institutional service, two deaths — and who is, despite all of it, no closer to the answer to the question he has never been able to answer: what, beneath the borrowed templates, is left of Simon Williams. The middle period is the long demonstration that the question cannot be answered by accumulation of experience. The next period will demonstrate what happens when a man stops trying to answer it and begins, instead, to construct grievances against the world that failed to answer it for him.

*End segment. Continued in Chronology IV.*


## Chronology IV — Recent History & Current State

# CHRONOLOGY IV — Recent History & Current State

## Subject: SIMON WILLIAMS (a/k/a "Wonder Man," "Wondy" [intimates only], SAG-AFTRA member in good standing under birth name)
## Period covered: approx. the last decade of the published record through present-day observation window.

---

### IV.1 — Entry conditions to the present era

Subject enters the current era already carrying the accumulated load detailed in Chronologies I–III: two confirmed deaths, one extended discorporation, the ionic instability that began manifesting in the late Bendis run, the Revengers humiliation (founded and led a strike on the Avengers Mansion; arrested; remanded; effectively a public meltdown narrated as ideology), and the slow, awkward reintegration that followed under Remender's *Uncanny Avengers* roster. He is, at point of entry, a man who has been very publicly wrong, in front of the people whose regard organizes his sense of self, and who has not yet metabolized it.

The behavioral baseline at entry: pacifism declared but not consistently held; ionic form stable in calm but flaring under affect; living transiently (no fixed lease of more than six months traceable for roughly four years); regular but discontinuous contact with Wanda Maximoff (largely one-sided on his end, polite-distant on hers); active but unsteady SAG-AFTRA work record (commercials, voice-over, two cable guest spots, one direct-to-streaming feature whose budget collapsed mid-production); no current treatment relationship with any mental-health provider that the file can confirm, though several attempts are documented and abandoned within four to six sessions each.

### IV.2 — The Hollywood reconstruction

The defining choice of the present era is the deliberate re-pivot to the screen career, framed by the subject in interviews and on his own intermittently-maintained social presence as "going back to what I'm actually good at." The framing is itself diagnostic — it concedes, without saying so, that costumed heroism has become a domain where he no longer trusts his own competence.

Concrete observations:

- **Representation.** Re-signed with a mid-tier agency after his prior representation dropped him during the Revengers indictment.
